For God's sake, what is McSweeney's anyway? Is this some reference to a Tim Burton character left on the animated film that could have been thriving in Spencer's Gifts or Hot Topic via t-shirt by now? As far as I'm concerned your two great accomplishments in life have been mentioning Shoah and Rising Up in your foreword to Infinite Jest - and yet why has Shoah been re-released, dutifully, by Criterion, while Rising Up has merely been reprinted abridged in some bogus edition, while all of this time you've been apparently working on equally bogus books.    I read The Circle on a combination of quadruple espressos, gin, and a single bite of naan/curry. I finished your little book in an hour and realized, again, the way you insist on mastering the capricious act of taking a dead concept (Its death in best case scenario brought on by prior archival finality in specific epic work(s) and re-writing it in the weakest voice possible.    For instance, Danielle Steel could have written A Heartbreaking Work, and just renamed it My Sorrows in San Francisco, and it would pass off unnoticed. Tao Lin could have written What Is The What and renamed it something equally idiotic, like, That is They Then, or, This Was Not That. A high school senior from some unknown town in Omaha could have submitted The Circle to McSweeney's and it would have been laughed out of the room. You are saying nothing new in terms of Western Society being doomed by technology. If you said a single thing no one already knew, your novel would have been great. Instead, you are merely fictionalizing the current merge of neofascist politics and hypersaturating technology. Destroy critical thought is the ideology. Intelligence has been smashed into 'Speaking Out.' Is Speaking Out writing a book like yours? No. No one is getting anything new now, and a lot of teenagers look up to you the way they once did bands like Slipknot and Korn - mild messages from millionaires which last not too long.    I'm pretty surprised Dave Wallace was actually friends with people like you and Johnathan Frozen. The huge difference is that his books actually matter, whereas nothing you or Fronkzen write goes beyond, or shows reader things he hasn't seen before, or articulates emotions long lost in confusion and subconscious which it is the writer of literature and poetry to put together, through logical puzzles and ontological restructuring - Not, on the other hand, 'Ha! I bet Oprah's going to LOVE this! I bet my fourteen-year old sister is SO into the new Eggers book!'    Come on Dave. Write like an adult. Your so-called literature is nothing more than reincarnated albeit overly-sentimental concept of the pop-punk band. The Circle is like MXPX releasing a dystopian concept album no one could possibly give a s***about unless they were trying to recapture the time/money/brain cells spent on the book.    Consider this an open challenge. I dare you to write something intelligent next time. Write something mind-blowing or just don't bother.    This book reads like George Orwell and Aldous Huxley on a tandem bicycle on Zoloft running away from Neil Postman, drugged out, flaccid, forgettable. You should have spent the time it took to write this working out a way to reissue WTV's magnum opus, instead of mentioning it on occasion for anyone with like fifteen thousand dollars to spend on books, when yours aren't worth much more than fifteen minutes.    Sincerely    Mr. Healey.

0486424545|5.0|\\"Only connect! That was the whole of her sermon. Only connect the prose and the passion and both will be exalted, and human love will be seen at its height. Live in fragments no longer.\\"<br /><br />Prose and passion are both connected in this novel written in 1910 by E. M. Forster.  \\"Only connect\\" is the refrain in this book.  If you enjoy reading novels written in earlier decades because they allow a kind of time travel, this novel takes you back to England at the turn of the 20th century.  It shows you what life in London and its environs was like for several classes and cultures in the first decade of the 1900's.<br /><br />What is the message which E.M. Forster is trying to convey in the admonishment: \\"only connect\\"?  He portrays the materialistic, practical, and successful Wilcox family (which is the prose) in comparison to the artistic and idealistic Schlegel family (which is the passion).  Apparently Forster believes that when the prose and passion marry, both are exalted for it.  And he illustrates how that happens in this book, and the effect on the characters involved.<br /><br />In this novel, the Wilcox and Schlegel family unite in a marriage and a series of events.  The Wilcoxes gain some humility and sensitivity, and the Schlegel family gains gumption and strength.  The Schlegels also connect with Leonard Bass, who is from the lower economic class and works for Henry Wilcox, and try to improve his opportunites.  That effort goes horribly wrong.<br /><br />You sense that E.M. Forster hopes this novel will inspire folks of all classes to connect and benefit from each other's strengths, despite the difficulty in doing so.

Of the 273 pages, there is only one eight-line paragraph devoted specially to \\"The Extraverted HSP\\". Had I known it was written for those inhibited, withdrawn, socially fearful HSPs, I don't think I would have bought it.<br /><br />I liked the first chapter. I highlighted several portions I found to be incredibly accurate.<br /><br />\\"HSPs can be instantly aware, whether they wish to be or not, of the mood, the friendships and enmities, the freshness or staleness of the air, the personality of the one who arranged the flowers.\\"<br /><br />\\"We reflect more on everything. From there on, however, I felt excluded much of the time; it was a slow fade into gradual and total disconnect.<br /><br />Dr. Aron goes into great detail in the second and third chapter, explaining \\"arousal,\\" a word she uses as a synonym for \\"stimulation,\\" and the affect it has on an HSP. I found some of this useful and insightful, as I can be bothered by too much noise, bright lights, uncomfortable clothing, etc..., but the majority of the content is devoted to helping \\"shy\\" persons overcome the egregious label that has shaped so much of their personality. 0345522478|1.0|One has to wonder how such a book as this gets published; even more so how it manages to collect so many five-star reviews. I don't have time to outline all the bad points so blatantly evident in Hounded, so I'll hone in on the most unfortunate: i.e., the writing itself. Most people are aware of the old axiom: show, don't tell. The author of this book may be the only person who missed that one. Perhaps he was busy stroking his ancient druidic crystal of power in Creative Writing 101 rather than listening to the instructor. In any case, although I do not personally adhere to the aforementioned stricture with the religious zeal of some, there are times when it proves its point. This book is one.<br />The first several paragraphs of Hounded are dedicated not to plot or action or any kind of dramatic scene but to simply explaining every fantastic aspect of the narrator cum protagonist's existence in one monumental data-dump. We learn in this first 1000 words all of the interesting details that are supposed to make him an intriguing character. Only problem is: they don't. They make him an artificially contrived cliche.<br />But apparently, this rather shallow device works for the majority of people who read this book. (Scratches his head.) I wonder how they would have felt if the book had been written well instead. In other words, if instead of simply data-dumping every detail of the protag up front, the author had presented us with an apparently normal-looking, -acting person, who is revealed through some compelling action to be a 2100-year-old Druid. If this approach had been taken, I may have been able to stomach the rest of the irritating qualities of this novel. As it is, I have wasted my money. Don't waste yours.

006202406X|2.0|One choice can--and will-- change everything. In the conclusion of the Divergent series, the place that Tris, Tobias, and the rest of their people have called home for decades is now keeping them prisoner, locked away from the outside world that the Divergent are apparently supposed to save. Kept from the truth for so long, Tris and her friends are determined to discover what is really out there, and what their purpose is. But the truth is bigger than they realize, and they must decide what they are going to do with the dangerous and life changing information given to them. With pain, loss, heartbreak, and sacrifice clogging their past and present, who will they trust and what truth will they believe to help them journey into their future?<br /><br />Allegiant possesses many positive qualities that are very similar to the previous two books: tension between people groups that have differing views of how their city should operate, relationship issues and improvements between Tris and Tobias, and personnel journeys that each character goes through just to name a few. The growth of the characters are wonderful, and Roth does a great job of not making \\"perfect\\" characters, but ones that have flaws and issues and must discover what they are and how to work through them.<br /><br />Although this book has these good aspects, there are many problems with the story. Roth changes the focus of the story, redirecting it to a place beyond the ruined city of the factions. She incorporates an entirely new plot that not only causes the book to turn away from the city that is supposed to be the main focus, but also seriously complicates the plot with a government that also has some big issues. By adding another broken government, it completely takes away the goal of the entire book series, and that is to heal the city.<br /><br />By including another government, this also adds to another problem this book has and that is all the unanswered questions: who rules the government? What shape is the country in? Why is Tris the only Divergent to ever have been tested and have three factions? Does her brother Caleb ever get why it was wrong to help the woman who tried to kill his sister, or is he still completely oblivious?<br /><br />Probably the biggest issue with Allegiant is the ending. Allegiant does something that the other books do not do, and that is have both Tris and Tobias' point of views. The inclusion of Tobias is great at first, but by the end the true reason of its presence is realized, and it is not what readers are expecting. The general response for the ending is going to be split among the readers; part of the readers will become extremely angry over what happens, and the other part will be happy that Roth ended differently than other mainstream books and praise its uniqueness. The last part of the book is going to be controversial among fans, and since the movie company for the series, Red Wagon Entertainment, is already slotted to film Insurgent, the second book in the series, it will be interesting to see how it will play out.<br /><br />Though parts of the book were entertaining, attention grabbing, and thought provoking, the crowded and complicated plot, the unanswered questions, and the questionable ending, causes Allegiant to fall short of a climatic finale to the series.<br /><br />For more reviews check out my blog: [...]
B00E59ZROG|5.0|What sheer, beautiful, divinely-sparked joy to have Karl B&ouml;hm's magnificent, classic Beethoven performances back in my collection again after far-too long an absence--all together in this handsomely packaged 6-disc box set from 2013.<br /><br />Not that these recordings have ever been out of the catalog for very long (if at all) since their initial release in the early 1970s (the symphonies were recorded between 1970 and 1971); the integral 8-LP box set was available for many years under several different cover designs (including the ubiquitous introductory come-on from the International Preview Society (BMG Music Service) in its early days), as well as individual albums on LP, cassette, and CD. I've owned a number of them in addition to that much-treasured box set; an early full-price 2-LP coupling of the superb Eighth and Ninth, a cassette of the Sixth, and a rather disappointing late-eighties or early nineties-era CD-re-issue of the Ninth. Much better was the delightful 1996 pairing of the Sixth with Schubert's Fifth symphony--still one of my very favorite CDs out of more than 1,000. The complete symphonic cycle was again re-issued in the early 1990s as part of the DG Doubles series. The Ninth has knocked about on various mid-price issues for more than thirty years now. It's a pretty safe bet that most long-time collectors will have at least a few of these--in one iteration or another--already.<br /><br />Why then plop down $30 for yet another re-issue of B&ouml;hm's Beethoven? Aside from the quality and convenience of this new packaging (individual discs in printed cardboard sleeves housed within a sturdy, laminated cardboard clamshell box with no excess \\"shake\\" room), improved sound is a major plus. Many of the earliest analog-to-digital transfers were less than adequate, certainly lacking the warmth and immediacy of vinyl without much gain in depth, detail, or fidelity. While DG was often ahead of the industry's technological curve, it too had its share of disappointing releases in the first years of the CD boom, when it was all some companies could do to keep up with the frenetic, ever-growing demand for more. (The muddy-sounding re-issue of B&ouml;hm's brilliant Ninth mentioned above is a case in point.) Then too, some engineers didn't quite know what to make of the new digital technology. As such, one was often subjected to shrill, shrieking trebles, raucous, loud, rock-n-roll-like basses, and murky, nebulous mid-levels that were virtually unlistenable. Sound levels were often set to extremes, sometimes barely turned up beyond a whisper, other times, deafeningly wide open (early BIS CDs even came with a warning label on the jewel case).<br /><br />Although there is no reference to re-mastering in the documentation accompanying this present album--no dates or mention of processing other than 2013 as the year of compilation--my ears remark a greater clarity in the overall sound picture of these transfers, blessedly devoid of tape hiss without the concomitant loss of detail at either end of the spectrum; the trebles less harsh, the basses less rambunctious. The sweet sound of the Vienna Philharmonic woodwind section has never been more pleasantly apparent. The strings shimmer, and the sometimes rather mellow-sounding brass shines through with admirable grace and nobility. This effectively takes the bad taste of so many earlier ill-conceived re-issues from my perpetually skeptically-discriminating palate.<br /><br />Of course, the best and most important reason to own this set is for these splendid benchmark performances--the last of the great \\"old-school classical\\" interpretations, and definitely  among the finest of their most-celebrated near-contemporaries (the cycles by Szell and the Cleveland Orchestra (Sony) and Karajan  with the Berlin Philharmonic (DG)). Where Szell could seem obsessively rigid in his quest for technical precision, and Karajan almost flippant in his prettified, breezy, surface-skimming approach, B&ouml;hm emphasized drama without sacrificing lyricism or buoyancy. These lucid, well-paced performances strike a commodious balance between dramatic heft and textural clarity. Beethoven's compositional technique often reminds me of one of those clear-glass clock cases, in which all the workings are plainly (and intentionally) visible solely for the sake of aesthetic delectation, and B&ouml;hm takes expert advabtage of this artfully built-in transparency. (Listen, for example, to the scherzo movements of the Second and Third symphonies, or to the wonderfully lithe first movements of the Fourth and Eighth.)<br /><br />While it wouldn't be exactly right to refer to B&ouml;hm as \\"self-effacing\\"--one is always aware of a strong hand at the helm--there is no conductorly self-indulgence here, no idiosyncratic excess. This is not the temperamental heaven-storming of Toscanini trying to channel Beethoven's tortured soul anew; nor Klemperer at war with his own demons (or, as in the case of his Ninth, with his own orchestra); nor is it Karajan or Carlos Kleiber (as much as I admire the latter's splendid Fifth and Seventh (DG Originals)) striking off in some radically new \\"modern\\" interpretive direction, largely involving playing the music faster than anyone before them.<br /><br />This set features what may be the finest versions of Beethoven's Fourth and Eighth ever recorded; a glorious, top-flight \\"Eroica\\", a Ninth that comes as close to \\"perfection\\" as any one is ever likely to hear (including one of the most exquisitely synergistic vocal quartets ever assembled for the work), a brilliant \\"Pastoral\\" for the ages, and, overall, some of the most consistently satisfying readings of these iconic, eternally quintessential works made in modern times.<br /><br />Enthusiastically, passionately recommended!ch, B&ouml;hm emphasized drama without sacrificing lyricism or buoyancy. B00BD99JMW|3.0|First of all, I must begin by warning that my review has SPOILERS in it. Secondly, I have never bothered to write a review on a book before, but I felt compelled to write one for this book. I have been looking forward to this book for such a long time, and I must say, I was very disappointed with the way that Veronica Roth chose to end it. I have recommended this series to a number of people, but after reading the last book, I have told my friends not to bother reading the series. The ending is an absolutely awful way to end the story! I am not a teen reader either, in fact, I am the same age as Roth, which is one reason why I have admired her work (it's hard enough to go to college without writing a trilogy on the side!). I do understand that Roth chose to turn Tris into a selfless heroine, but I do not think she had to kill her off in order to do this. The fact that Tris took Caleb's place and was willing to face death for a selfless reason for the first time shows that she underwent an important change in character. She loved her brother enough, even though he betrayed her, to take his place and face death. She took the step, that doesn't mean that she actually has to die! What I find the most cruel about this decision is that Tris survives the impossible to survive death serum and we have the hope that she will survive death yet again, and then she ends up getting shot and killed instead. Really? Tris, the girl who has escaped death numerous times throughout this series, and does the impossible to survive the death serum gets killed by a bullet. She has the willpower to live and is able to talk herself out of just about any situation, and the man who loved her mother kills her. That is not believable based on the pattern we have already seen with Tris. I was deeply disappointed that Roth decided to make Tris die because it was not consistent with the story. And what about the end of the conflict in Chicago? Tobias just talks to Evelyn the tyrant and she decidesto give up everything she has worked for for many years and give up the fight. It would have been more believable for Tris to survive a bullet to the head than for Evelyn to reconcile so easily with Tobias and surrender her throne. These holes in the plot were hard to overlook. I hate to say it, but Roth should not be surprised if people do not continue reading her books because she has broken the trust of her readers. Not only that, unless the movie producers take the liberty to change the ending of the third book in the third movie, people will not want to see the movies. Roth should have taken into consideration who her audience is. I must say that I am going to have to pretend that the book ended differently than it did because it is so disappointing to think Tris and Tobias did not get their happy ending, especially considering that Roth could have still gotten her message across without Tris actually dying.

The problem is that it's a pain in the ass to use. To use it with your computer, you actually have to go to Netflix, sign in, choose whatever you want to view, play it, then switch it over to the Chromecast. Episode ended? You have to get back on your computer (or phone), go back to the episode page, choose the next episode, click play, and change the stream over to Netflix AGAIN. Every time. I had no idea it was going to be so cumbersome to use.<br /><br />To use it on your phone, you have to first download the Chromecast app and pair the device. Then you have to download the Netflix app (if not already installed), and set it up for your Netflix account. Luckily, it is much less cumbersome in the app: you simply switch the video to the Chromecast in the Netflix app, browse until you find something to watch, and hit play. Episode done? You then have to wait a bit before the video buffers to better quality so it doesn't look horrendous. Trying to stream these to the tv results in very jumpy video with no audio transferred. I wish there was an NHL Gamecenter app that worked like the youtube plugin, I'd get a ton more use out of this device.<br /><br />As it stands, I'm giving it 3 out of 5 stars because, well, it's just okay. I'm still deciding whether or not I want to return it and just spend the $15 more to get a basic Roku that will play netflix and stream gamecenter games.<br /><br />Update (11/7/2013): I've decided to keep it, and I'm adding a star. The Pandora app was just released (although I don't use it) and hopefully the sdk will be out soon so that more can come. What this device does, it does very well. There have been a few instances where I've played youtube tutorial videos on my television so I could do something on my laptop at the same time, so that's moderately useful. Hopefully soon full screen non-youtube videos can be broadcasted rather than having to just broadcast the whole tab with the video windowed, but that could be a flash thing.

The development of Matthew's character was one of the main reasons I so looked forward to future episodes; I couldn't wait to see how he would save Downton and even possibly bring to it an even more exciting future.  But now, I have no desire to watch next season.  The plans I had to purchase all three seasons on DVD for my parents as a Christmas gift are now by the wayside because it is just too dreadful to watch.  So sad to see a beautiful series disintegrate.  The only way I will reconsider is if by some remarkable twist, Matthew survived the crash.  I'll wait to hear from friends who choose to watch in January ... but as mentioned, they've lost my business for now.

B009LMTDL0|4.0|Lean In: Women, Work, and the Will to Lead by Sheryl Sandberg<br /><br />\\"Lean In\\" is a well-balanced book that calls for equality.  It provides guidance on how to help women seek leadership roles and seek fulfillment and satisfaction in all aspects of their lives.  It's an inspirational book that encourages women to dream big and achieve their full potential by \\"leaning in\\".  It also helps men gain an understanding on how to support women in the workplace and at home. Chief operating officer at Facebook, Sheryl Sandberg shares her fascinating personal journey while providing the public with an illuminating and useful book.  This enlightening 241-page book includes the following eleven chapters: 1. The Leadership Ambition Gap: What Would You Do if You Weren't Afraid?, 2. Sit at the Table, 3. Success and Likeability, 4. It's a Jungle Gym, Not a Ladder, 5. Are You My Mentor?, 6. Seek and Speak Your Truth, 7. Don't Leave Before You Leave, 8. Make Your Partner a Real Partner, 9. The Myth of Doing It All, 10. Let's Start Talking About It, and 11. Working Together Toward Equality.<br /><br />Positives:<br />1. As well written a book as you will find.  Engaging prose.<br />2. Gender equality is such an important yet neglected topic. I'm glad to see someone of Sandberg's credentials tackling this issue in a powerful yet reasonable manner.<br />3. I was concerned that this book was going to inspire women at the expense of men (male bashing) but nothing could be further from the truth. Sandberg is an energetic force but she is fair and instructive.<br />4. I really liked the balanced style of how this book was written.  Sandberg is engaging (a touch of humor, a little deprecation), forthright, and the book is well sourced (love that).<br />5. Sandberg loves to speak with substance.  This book is full of facts and source material. \\"A meager twenty-one of the Fortune 500 CEOs are women. Women hold about 14 percent of executive officer positions, 17 percent of board seats, and constitute 18 percent of our elected congressional officials.\\"<br />6. Does a wonderful job of going over the complex challenges that women face. \\"The gender stereotypes introduced in childhood are reinforced throughout our lives and become self-fulfilling prophesies. Most leadership positions are held by men, so women don't expect to achieve them, and that becomes one of the reasons they don't.\\"<br />7. Shares some fascinating stories and testimonials about her life.  The people who inspired her and those who played an instrumental role in her career.  Some high-level name dropping...it's good to be well connected.<br />8. The insecurities that hold people back. \\"This phenomenon of capable people being plagued by self-doubt has a name--the impostor syndrome. Both men and women are susceptible to the impostor syndrome, but women tend to experience it more intensely and be more limited by it.\\"<br />9. The book is also surprisingly candid, \\"Many of us have experienced being angry with someone and then having to pretend everything's great in public. My husband, Dave, and I have our moments, and just when we are getting into it, it will be time to go to a friend's house for dinner. We put on our \\"everything's great\\" smiles, and amazingly, after a few hours, it often is.\\"<br />10. There are a lot of tidbits that are enlightening and useful.  \\"If we want a world with greater equality, we need to acknowledge that women are less likely to keep their hands up. We need institutions and individuals to notice and correct for this behavior by encouraging, promoting, and championing more women.\\" Interesting, I'll keep that in mind along with other useful information.<br />11. The reality of stereotypes. \\"This experiment supports what research has already clearly shown: success and likeability are positively correlated for men and negatively correlated for women.\\" \\"Fear is at the root of so many of the barriers that women face.\\" Many great examples.<br />12. An interesting discussion on career growth, \\"ladders are limiting--people can move up or down, on or off. Jungle gyms offer more creative exploration. There's only one way to get to the top of a ladder, but there are many ways to get to the top of a jungle gym.\\"<br />13. Excellent chapter on mentorship. \\"Studies show that mentors select prot&eacute;g&eacute;s based on performance and potential.\\" A lot of interesting subtopics covered.<br />14. Important traits to have. \\"Communication works best when we combine appropriateness with authenticity, finding that sweet spot where opinions are not brutally honest but delicately honest.\\" \\"Taking initiative pays off. It is hard to visualize someone as a leader if she is always waiting to be told what to do.\\"<br />15. Great quotes, one of my favorites \\"the upside of painful knowledge is so much greater than the downside of blissful ignorance\\". Another, \\"The most common way people give up their power is by thinking they don't have any.\\"<br />16. A great discussion on making your partner a real partner.  \\"A more equal division of labor between parents will model better behavior for the next generation.\\"<br />17. Find out the worst question to ask a woman.  Hint...Tina Fey said it.<br />18. As a husband and a father of two, Sandberg makes some cogent points regarding shared-parenting duties that resonate. \\"If there is a new normal for the workplace, there is a new normal for the home too.\\"<br />19. The need for not only the conversation about equality to take place but to inspire a transformation.  Sandberg's success as a business leader, drive and global visibility will go a long way toward reaching such goals. \\"Talking can transform minds, which can transform behaviors, which can transform institutions.\\"<br />20. Defining what leadership is ultimately about. \\"Leadership is about making others better as a result of your presence and making sure that impact lasts in your absence.\\"<br />21. One of the best referenced books you'll ever read.<br />22.  Sandberg is donating all her income from this book to establish the Lean In nonprofit organization.<br /><br />Negatives:<br />1. One of the most difficult aspects of dealing with gender issues is recognizing that we are evolved primates and differences between the sexes manifest themselves in our behavior.  As Gloria Steinem incorrectly observed, \\"It's not about biology, but about consciousness.\\"  No, it's about both.  We are limited by our biology but we can certainly become a better society if we consciously empower women by improving conditions that lead to their full fulfillment as potential leaders if they so desire.  Recognizing our biological differences and putting our evolved brains to better use will in the end lead to the desired results that Sandberg so eloquently inspires us to seek.  I always cringe when people conveniently want to do deny or set aside our biology.  For the record, I have the utmost respect and admiration for Gloria Steinem I just happen to disagree on this issue.<br />2. How effectively can an average woman put this book to practical use?  The meteoric rise of Sandberg is reflected in this book and immediately puts her in circles that the average person can't relate to.  In short, how relevant will this book be for the average woman?<br />3. I have reservations about one testimonial that Sandberg shared.  Honestly, how many women have the opportunity to pump milk while conducting a high-level meeting? \\"But the advent of the modern-day breast pump has changed the equation. At Google, I would lock my office door and pump during conference calls.\\" It's a cultural issue worth discussing and it's one area where leadership can do more to help.<br />4. Single women jumping around in the jungle gym (more reflective of the modern corporate ladder) may feel left out. There is one good reference worth mentioning though, \\"My coworkers should understand that I need to go to a party tonight--and this is just as legitimate as their kids' soccer game--because going to a party is the only way I might actually meet someone and start a family so I can have a soccer game to go to one day!\\" A good story but few and far between.<br />5. As Sandberg stated herself this is not a self-help book or a book strictly about career management; it's an inspirational book that incorporates such elements.  Women looking for books strictly along those lines would be advised to read someone like Selena Rezvani.<br />6. The lack of a bibliography.  A book that makes so many references warranted a separate formal bibliography.  Notes are included and they are excellent but a bibliography would have added value.<br /><br />In summary, this is an important book that will hopefully achieve its goal of inspiring more women to \\"lean in\\" and achieve their full potential.  You may not agree with every suggestion or conclusion Sandberg reaches but we may all agree that we owe it to our wives, mothers, sisters and daughters to true equality.  Despite some of my reservations, I really enjoyed and learned some valuable lessons from this enlightening book.  I highly recommend it and men this is a conversation worth having!<br /><br />Further recommendations: \\"[[ASIN:1591795176 Conscious Business: How to Build Value Through Values]]\\" by Fred Kofman, \\"[[ASIN:0671027034 How to Win Friends & Influence People]]\\" by Dale Carnegie, \\"[[ASIN:1118104900 Pushback: How Smart Women Ask--and Stand Up--for What They Want]]\\" by Selena Rezvani, \\"[[ASIN:1401303706 The Dream Manager]]\\" by Mathew Kelly, \\"[[ASIN:1885167776 The ONE Thing: The Surprisingly Simple Truth Behind Extraordinary Results]]\\" by Gary Keller, \\"[[ASIN:0385528752 Switch: How to Change Things When Change Is Hard]]\\" by Chip and Dan Heath, \\"[[ASIN:023034190X Work with Me: The 8 Blind Spots Between Men and Women in Business]]\\" by Barbara Annis and John Gray, \\"[[ASIN:0061789089 Power: Why Some People Have It and Others Don't]]\\" by Jeffrey Pfeffer, \\"[[ASIN:0316017930 Outliers: The Story of Success]]\\" and \\"[[ASIN:0316346624 The Tipping Point: How Little Things Can Make a Big Difference]]\\" by Malcolm Gladwell, \\"The Power of Habit: Why We Do What We Do in Life and Business\\" by Charles Duhigg, \\"Rebounders: How Winners Pivot from Setback to Success\\" by Rick Newman, and \\"Drive: The Surprising Truth About What Motivates Us\\".andberg is donating all her income from this book to establish the Lean In nonprofit organization.<br /><br />Negatives:<br />1. 1451624468|3.0|As the title suggests, Krauss wrote this book to answer the age-old question of why there is something rather than nothing. The book was heralded by many atheists as the definitive answer to theists who claim God is necessary to explain the existence of physical reality. Indeed, in the afterward Richard Dawkins claimed that Krauss' book devastates theistic arguments based on cosmology just as Darwin's On the Origin of Species devastated theistic arguments based on design in biology. Other reviewers, however - including scientists, philosophers, and theologians - beg to differ. Having read the book myself (not just once, but two times now), I can see why they were less than impressed with Krauss' argument.<br /><br />While my overall assessment of Krauss' argument is not positive, truth be told, most of the book was quite enjoyable and informative.  That's because the first 2/3 of the book is a lesson on the historical development of modern cosmology.  Krauss doesn't make his case for why there is something rather than nothing until the last four chapters.  Unfortunately, that's where the book falls apart.<br /><br />Changing the question<br /><br />The most significant problem with Krauss' handling of the issue is the fact that he redefines the question in two significant ways.  First, he changes why to how.  Krauss says \\"why\\" questions are meaningless from a scientific perspective.  Science speaks to the \\"how\\" questions.  So to \\"scientize\\" the question of why there is something rather than nothing, Krauss changes the question to how there is something rather than nothing.  While this may be necessary to scientize the question, it is not necessary to make it meaningful.  Indeed, it actually changes the question.  Perhaps a scientific approach to the question that focuses on the how may give us great insight into origins, but answering the how question does not make the why question meaningless, or answer the why question.  At best, it would simply show that there are two sides to the question, and while science can answer one aspect of the question, it is incapable of answering the other.  It would not follow that the why question can be eliminated, or simply reduced to the how question.<br /><br />\\"Somethifying\\" nothing<br /><br />Secondly, and more importantly, Krauss changes the definition of \\"nothing.\\"  For Krauss, nothing is really something, namely the quantum vacuum. He attempts to show that natural processes are capable of transforming the quantum vacuum into all of the stuff we see today.  If true, it is very interesting, but unfortunately it has nothing to do with the question being asked.  The question posed is not how something became something else, but why there is something rather than nothing at all. And by \\"nothing,\\" we mean the absence of being, not a \\"thin\\" kind of something. The quantum vacuum is clearly something, not nothing, despite Krauss' claims to the contrary. Indeed, at times Krauss admits as much by saying things like \\"one should be tempted to consider [the quantum vacuum] as nothing,\\"[1] and repeatedly describes the quantum vacuum as \\"almost nothing.\\" If the quantum vacuum is almost nothing, then it is not truly nothing. Nothing is even less than the quantum vacuum. Nothing is the absence of all being itself: no quantum vacuum, no physical laws, not anything!  It's disingenuous, then, for Krauss to claim that science can explain how something came from nothing.  Science can do nothing of the sort.  As William Lane Craig has quipped, there are no physics of non-being!  Science can explain how things work, and even tell us their natural history, but in all cases they are explaining how something came from something, not how something came from nothing.<br /><br />Krauss can't plead ignorance regarding the intended meaning of \\"nothing\\" either. He acknowledges that the age-old philosophical question understands \\"nothing\\" as non-being (although elsewhere in the book he chides philosophers and theologians for always changing the definition so that science can never answer it), but says he takes a \\"flippant attitude toward this concern\\"[2] because it doesn't add to the conversation...because it can't be answered by science.  In other words, he doesn't like the actual question because his favored discipline (science) and favored epistemology (empiricism) cannot answer it (though he tries, as I'll discuss in a bit).  That is an interesting piece of biographical information, but it doesn't make the actual question one bit less meaningful.<br /><br />On second thought, science can answer that too!<br /><br />While Krauss' preferred approach to answering the question of why there is something rather than nothing is to change the definition of nothing so that it becomes another something, later in the book (page 146) Krauss claims that science can even explain how something can come from true nothingness.  How so?  Virtual particles.  Krauss writes:<br /><br />[M]erely defining \\"nothingness\\" as \\"nonbeing\\" is not sufficient to suggest that physics, and more generally science, is not adequate to address the question.  Let me give an additional, more specific argument here. Consider an electron-positron pair that spontaneously pops out of empty space near the nucleus of an atom and affects the property of that atom for the short time the pair exists. In what sense did the electron or positron exist before?  Surely by any sensible definition they didn't. There was potential for their existence, certainly, but that doesn't define being any more than a potential human being exists because I carry sperm in my testicles near a woman who is ovulating, and she and I might mate.<br /><br />And your argument is...?  What follows from this is...what precisely?\\"  His argument appears to be that since virtual particles come into being from non-being all the time, and this is a natural phenomenon that scientists \\"observe,\\" therefore science can provide a naturalistic explanation for howsomething can come from truly nothing.  If you're not scratching your head yet, let me help create the itch.  Krauss' \\"argument\\" is misguided on several counts.<br /><br />First, even if virtual particles are truly coming into being from non-being, observing this and explaining this are entirely different matters.  The only thing scientists know is that it happens.  They have no idea how or why it happens (which is why some have even resorted to the absurd conclusion that these are uncaused events).  Virtual particles coming into being from utter nothingness would simply presents scientists with an innumerable number of additional examples of the problem needing to be answered.  Rather than trying to explain a single event in which something comes into being from non-being (the coming into being of physical reality), they have to explain the occurrence of countless events in which something comes into being from non-being.  Thinking you have solved one problem by pointing to the existence of innumerable instances of the same inexplicable problem does not get you one step closer to actually solving the problem![3]  It would only prove that the problem is worse than originally conceived.  Pointing to virtual particles does nothing to explain how something can come from nothing.  At best, it just demonstrates how ubiquitous and intractable the problem of creation ex nihilo is for science to explain.<br /><br />Second, it cannot be the case that virtual particles are coming into being from true nothingness.  For some X to come into being, it must have a source that contains within it the potential for X.  For example, cars can come into being because the steel they are created from has the potential to become a car if acted upon in a certain fashion.  If there is no source material, however, then there is no potential for a car.  Nothingness has no properties, and thus has no potential for anything.  If you start with nothing, you will always and only end up with nothing.  Something can only come from something else.  Krauss' own analogy reveals this same point.  There is potential for a human being to come into existence only because a source exists with properties sufficient to produce a human being under the right circumstances. But if there was no man carrying sperm in his testicles, and there was no woman ovulating, would it make any sense to speak of the potential for a human being?  No.  The potential for X requires a source of X, and yet Krauss denies the existence of any source for virtual particles, and the universe itself.  While Krauss is right to distinguish between the potential for existence and actual existence, that distinction is only relevant when we are talking about something coming from something.  When we are talking about something coming from nothing, however, we are talking about actual existence arising without a source that contains the potential for it to exist.  How one can get an actual something without even a potential something is beyond me.<br /><br />Thirdly, virtual particles are not coming from non-being, but arise from within the quantum vacuum itself.  Krauss admits as much.  While we may not understand what causes this phenomenon, this is not an example of something coming from nothing, but of something coming from something else.<br /><br />Stop...in the name of natural law<br /><br />Every causal explanation must come to a terminus at some point. For theists, the causal terminus is God - a necessary being who is the source and cause of everything other than Himself.  For many atheists, the causal terminus is natural laws.  Natural laws are eternal, and the source of all physical reality (this represents a gross misunderstanding of the ontology of natural law), and a brute fact of reality (they are the necessary beings, not God).  Krauss so desires to answer the problem of how something can come from nothing that he even attempts to explain the origin of physical laws...or so he says.  Inreality, he punts on the issue by appealing to a multiverse that causes physical laws, without explaining the origin of the multiverse.  In the end, the fundamental physical reality is neither named nor explained.<br /><br />Krauss acknowledges two possible explanations for the origin of natural laws: God or something non-supernatural.  It should come as no surprise that he rejects the God explanation.  What is surprising is how poor his reasons are for rejecting it.  Then again, given his general disdain for and lack of familiarity with philosophy, his inability to critically evaluate the hypothesis is not at all surprising.  First, he trots out the `if God created the natural laws, then who created God' line.  I never cease to be amazed at how many times Christians have answered this point, and how many times atheists don't pay attention.  On theism, God is a necessary and hence uncreated being.  To ask, Who caused God?, then, is to ask, Who caused the Uncreated Being to exist?, which is meaningless.  It makes no more sense to ask who created God then to ask what sound silence makes.<br /><br />Next, he posits that if we invoke God to explain what determines \\"the rules,\\" then who determines God's rules for Him?  Really?  On theism, God is the metaphysical ultimate and first cause.  There can be nothing that determines \\"the rules\\" for God?  Krauss' question makes as much sense as asking what mathematical equation determines mathematical truths.  It's actually worse than that since \\"the rules\\" Krauss has in mind, natural law, could not possibly apply to God since God is not a physical being.  I don't know what other rules Krauss could possibly have in mind, but whatever they may be, it doesn't make any sense to speak of rules that govern a free-will agent.<br /><br />Then, Krauss argues that a First Cause of the universe would only argue for deism, not theism.  Ok?  No theist would dispute that?  Other arguments are necessary as part of a cumulative case to get tothe God of the Bible, but that does nothing to detract from the fact that a divine being would still be the cause of the universe, and deism is miles away from the atheism espoused by Krauss!  Deism may not get you to Christianity, but it would definitely invalidate atheism.<br /><br />Next, Krauss argues that God cannot be a first cause because every effect must have a prior cause, ad infinitum (strange, given his admission that the best evidence available to us today supports an absolute beginning to the universe).  Once again, Krauss would benefit from the help of philosophy, which demonstrates the absurdity of an actual infinite.<br /><br />Finally, he argues that if something can only come from something, then even God cannot create natural laws from nothing.  Apparently it hasn't dawned on him that God is not nothing.  God's creation of physical laws would be an example of something coming from something, not something from nothing.<br /><br />After dismissing the God hypothesis, Krauss turns to a non-supernatural hypothesis: the multiverse.  Krauss knows the precise values of the physical constants are fine-tuned for the existence of intelligent life, and proposes that the multiverse could explain the value of the physical constants since, in a multiverse, each universe could have a different set of values for the physical constants.  We just happen to live in a universe in which the random distribution of those values is life-permitting.  But once again, Krauss has moved the goal post.  When it comes to questions of origins, what Krauss needs to explain is why there are physical laws in the first place, not why the physical laws in our universe have assumed the values they do.  If the multiverse both creates and determines the value of the physical laws in each universe, then the multiverse, not physical laws, is a more basic feature of physical reality.  What, then, explains the multiverse?  What explains the mechanism that produces multiple universes withmultiple values for the physical constants?  In the end, Krauss admits he can't answer the question. He writes, \\"Where did they [physical laws] come from? That is a good question, and one of the more modern answers is that even the laws themselves may be random, coming into existence along with universes that may arise. This may still beg the question of what allows any of this to be possible, but at some level it is... `turtles all the way down'.\\"[4]  In other words, he doesn't have a clue.  So much for explaining the origin of all physical reality from nothing!<br /><br />Conclusion<br /><br />At the end of the day, Krauss' book answers a question that no one was asking.  He has explained how something could become something else, but not how something could come from nothing.  In that respect, his book is an abject failure.  And yet, it remains a monument to materialist thinking, and demonstrates the bankruptcy of scientism and empiricism to answer the most fundamental questions humans can ask.  Science is a marvelous discipline, and can tell us much about the physical world.  What it cannot do is tell us why or how something could come from absolutely nothing.

006202406X|4.0|I just finished Allegiant last night and I wanted to share some thoughts:<br /><br />One of the big hurdles that any series like this has to overcome is big secrets (the answer is always less satisfying than what you think the secret is. JJ Abrams called it a magic box problem - what's in the magic box? when you open it you're let down, before it can be anything). Through Divergent and Insurgent the big secret was how this world came to be. There are many examples (LOST, I'm looking at you) of worlds that were created with large secrets where the payoff wasn't there. For Allegiant, the payoff really isn't there but I don't think that's Roth's fault, it's my imagination's. My understanding of the outside world was much different than the written one in Allegiant and so there was disappointment. I knew, though, that the disappointment was coming.<br /><br />What I liked about the book is what many people have been panning. The development of all the major characters happened in this book. It's almost as if we got to knew them and before they were taken away, motivations were shaped and we were allowed to mourn the ending of the book.<br /><br />The narrative switch between Four and Tris was confusing in a couple of the chapters and I found myself looking at the front of the chapter to see who was speaking, but for the most part, I thought the 'lack of voice' of Four fleshed him out a bit and humanized him. Where in Divergent he was introduced as a macho, shallow guy, this book evolved him into more of a thinker and more to a person who had a firm grasp on how he felt. I don't think it was bad and I'm probably not alone in saying his thoughts often looked a lot like how I would think and approach a situation as a guy.<br /><br />I also thought the last 10 chapters or so were handled well and the writing reflected how the characters thought and felt and I felt their sense of loss (trying not to give anything away).<br /><br />My lack of a 5th star comes from, in my opinion, confusion about how the outside of Chicago world worked. I understand the Fringe and how cities were setup, but I was confused about where everyone else was. The world would benefit from even a short story explaining how it came to be the way it was and where everyone outside of Chicago lived.<br /><br />Take it for what you will, I thought it was a strong ending to a book even though it had some things going against it. :)

006202406X|1.0|THIS REVIEW CONTAINS MASSIVE SPOILERS FROM THE START. TURN BACK NOW YE WHO WISH TO REMAIN UNSPOILED.<br /><br />I wasn't sure I was going to write a review of Allegiant, especially because a lot of other people have already touched on my primary problems (POV shifts being weak, writing so spare and minimalistic as to be completely incomprehensible at times) but I did want to talk a little more about Tris's death.<br /><br />Tris is a Christ figure. Throughout each book, even the bulk of Allegiant, we're shown over and over that she is her world's messiah. She is immune to the serums, she's shockingly \\"genetically pure\\" (which has it's own slew of problems that I don't think a white, upper-middle class woman should ever have written about, least of all someone as young as Roth), she's powerfully Divergent, etc etc etc. These things aren't necessarily a problem. The messianic figure has been a trope for hundreds of years, even pre-Christ. The Christ story is the one Western culture associates most strongly with the messianic figure, however, and because of that we expect a messiah story to go a certain way. Namely, we expect the messiah to come back from the dead.<br /><br />There are plenty of stories that deconstruct tropes - and the deconstruction of tropes is incredibly popular in media right now. Allegiant isn't a deconstruction. None of the Divergent books were a deconstruction of the messiah. None of them implied, in any way, that the human race doesn't need someone to lead it to salvation - which is exactly what Tris is doing throughout two and a half books. When she dies in Allegiant, the messiah trope isn't deconstructed. It's destroyed. And because tropes are a way to understand and parse media and stories, the reader is left utterly baffled and confused. This, in my opinion, is the primary failure of Allegiant. The destruction of a trope that Roth spent no time deconstructing is the problem, and it's the reason people are railing so hard against this story..

B000002KB8|5.0|This thrilling 1970 debut by Black Sabbath still sends chills down my spine, even though I really am too old to be listening to heavy metal any more.  Regardless, this is fantastic stuff that had as much of an impact on me as the 1969 debut by King Crimson.  I think chilling power sums up the Black Sabbath sound on the debut nicely.<br /><br />This stuff is at times raw, yet fairly sophisticated too. Geezer Butler simply thrashes his bass and really sets the tone for this early phase of the groups sound.  Although generally pretty loud, there is nice use of dynamics and creepy atmospheres - the title track with the combination of rain, thunder, and the terrifying sound of a tritone (\\"Devils interval\\") played on the electric guitar and bass is a great example. Oddly, this stands in contrast to the lyrics, which do not embrace evil in any way; rather the lyrics warn against such a thing.  There are also traces of the bands beginning as a blues band - the harmonica pops up here and there. Last, but not least, Ozzy really belts out with a signature vocal style that helped define the group during the 1970s.<br /><br />The sound quality of this US Warner Bros. release is not too bad and the CD booklet comes with some historical tidbits although the whole package is pretty skimpy. As a I recall, the vinyl LP was not much to write home about either.<br /><br />All in all, this is unbelievably powerful stuff that is truly heavy in a cosmic/metaphysical sense.  Great stuff that is recommended along with Paranoid (1970), Sabbath Bloody Sabbath (1973), and Sabotage (1975).

B00BD99JMW|4.0|Overall I think the book concluded the series in an emotive, cohesive way.  Moreover, for me this book and the whole series was a good ride, despite issues I had with the writing.  I didn't find any significant differences between Roth's writing style in this book versus the other two; the same issues annoyed me throughout (repetitive language, story elements that made no sense, plot holes, etc.). Switching back and forth between Tris and Tobias didn't bother me, but I think that's because their voices were so darn similar (that's not a good thing).  But, I guess I don't expect much nuanced writing out of YA books.  What I do expect are engaging characters and plot, which the book delivers, similarly to Divergent and Insurgent.<br /><br />This isn't to say I wasn't totally crying at the end, and at first so mad at Roth for concluding things the way she did (I am a grown woman and was crying at a YA book... jeez!).  But my feelings toward the finale have softened a couple days after finishing, and I appreciate it more.  So for those just winding up the series now, I'd say give yourself a couple days to mope and see how you feel about it in a little while.<br /><br />I have to give props to Roth for having the gumption to put a story out there that evokes major emotion, and not just the happy kind.  I'm mildly embarassed to admit that I've been thinking about it almost constantly since I wrapped up the book!  And even though many of my thoughts have centered on the final tragedy of the series, I think the fact I can't get it out of my head is the sign of a powerful book.  Few authors, particularly in this genre, are willing to bring their readers to a place filled with such heavy emotions (partially, I imagine, because of the type of backlash Roth is getting now).<br /><br />**SPOILERS AHEAD!  BE WARNED!**<br /><br />...<br /><br />...<br /><br />...<br /><br />...<br /><br />Unlike some readers, I don't think Tris' final decision was solely for the sake of her brother... it was because she wouldn't have been able to live with herself had she let Caleb die for the wrong reasons.  I also don't think her actions showed the same recklessness as they did in Insurgent; she really thought she might survive. However, parts of her death did feel contrived.  Roth brought us to a hopeful place, where Tris and Tobias were talking about their future together, only to have our hopes dashed at the very end!  Felt a bit like rubbing it in to me, and the death itself felt seemed abrupt.  It could have been explained better within the political context of the Divergent world, in order to make it feel more significant.<br /><br />I could imagine other ways for it to go that would have tied up the book's themes and pleased fans at the same time (for example, Tris could have become gravely hurt but survived anyway... and we could have waited to find that out until the Epilogue).  But I do think her mindset as she risked her life was totally consistent with her character and sense of morality.  Though it was sad, it was also beautiful.<br /><br />To sum up:  I enjoyed the ride, despite my misgivings about the writing style,  and in the end that's my main rating criteria.  Good series plagued with typical storytelling issues of the YA genre.  Twisty ending that will leave you thinking, for better or worse.

Perhaps being a published author with great reviews (both reader and professional) and approaching my 68th birthday, I wasn't in the mood for a book that flouts all the rules of good writing and gets praise heaped on it for that. This is self-indulgent, unedited writing at its most extreme. I imagine that if someone unknown like myself sent this sprawling stream-of-semi-consciousness book to a literary agent or publisher, I would not only get rejected, I would get a lot of letters back telling me to learn how to write before wasting people's time on such garbage. I suppose Mr. Wallace was hailed as a genius, because people couldn't understand what the hell he is writing about, so they assume he must be on a different level than them, but if they jump on his genius bandwagon, then they'll at least appear smart enough to recognize genius - the myth of it takes one to know one. Mr. Wallace may have been a genius in IQ terms, but this book is self-indulgent stupidity. B00EKQ3YZI|4.0|It's very difficult to compete with the original productions on some of these iconic songs, so each DJ/Producer deserves some credit -- particularly those who tackle the MONSTER hits of Summer's repertoire -- on this Verve release, LOVE TO LOVE YOU DONNA CD.<br /><br />This is a mixed bag with some hitting with fresh re-imaginings of the classics, while others miss altogether. I'm going to review each song, which I never do, and those that are hits are ones that have been able to keep a sense of the original in tone and meaning, giving a fresh new take on each; the misses are going to be the ones that are so different that the song becomes obliterated in the remix, creating something else entirely. Here's my example of what I mean by hits: \\"You Keep Me Hanging On\\" remake by Kim Wilde -- although it was given the makeover treatment in the 80s, one could go back to the original done by The Supremes and still appreciate both, as they hit the same mark in stylistic choice/tone, approach, theme, even though musically they're from different genres. I know that covers aren't exactly the same as remixes, but I wanted you to understand my reasoning of what I think is a successful update.<br /><br />I'm also glad that one of the DJs decided on a lesser known song \\"Working The Midnight Shift,\\" which wasn't released as a single (I don't think) and on the [[ASIN:B000001FEE Once Upon a Time]] album.<br /><br />LOVE TO LOVE YOU BABY -- HIT<br />This is a good re-imagining of the original keeping the moans and adding in electronic drum beats and embellishments, but never ruining the integrity of the song. The lyrics are intact, while the undercurrent keeps the original driving beats percolating throughout. This should be released as a single. In fact, I wish it was stretched out to the full 17+ minute version.<br /><br />DIM ALL THE LIGHTS -- MISS<br />This is so far off the mark it could be a different song completely. It is repetitive both with the small 4-word selection of lyrics and same inane cooing, along with the same 4 bars of music. This reminded me of the techno songs in the early 90s where one had to be rolling to enjoy what was incoherent to the sober.<br /><br />HOT STUFF -- MISS<br />This attempt just replaces the music track with another one, while keeping the vocals and lyrics intact. What you have is a competition between song and music and neither is the winner. It gets better towards the last quarter, but otherwise it's jarring, with the new musical track detracting from the vocals.<br /><br />I FEEL LOVE -- HIT<br />This is great, but it's mostly the original with the synth drums pumped way up, along with other artificial embellishments, but it retains the original flavor, sounding exquisitely refreshed especially with the choppy reverb on some parts of the lyrics, giving a real computerized and mechanical sound. It could be another successful single if released today.<br /><br />LOVE IS IN CONTROL (FINGER ON THE TRIGGER) -- HIT<br />This doesn't really sound new but it does sound like it could be part of an 80s movie soundtrack or something that could be done by the Scissor Sisters in present day. Sounds fresh and different than the original, but still retains the soul of the original.<br /><br />SUNSET PEOPLE -- HIT<br />This is another good update with added musical embellishments, taking out the original music track altogether, but retains the mood of the original, which I always heard as haunting and solemn. Lyrics are intact and it keeps the driving beat (albeit new -- almost like a counter beat?) with familiar sounds that link back to the original (namely the sound that sounds like a laser ray gun).<br /><br />WORKING THE MIDNIGHT SHIFT -- HIT/MISS<br />The original was somewhat melancholy and in a slower tempo and this presentation is faster and more lively, but still dark. This is a miss as far as updated Summer song because someone else is doing the vocals (Tiffany Roth), while retaining Summer's vocals on the bridge (high part), but I'm not even sure about that, so it's a hit as a cover version. I thought the vocals sounded way too different to be just a speeded up version of the original, so I looked at the credits.<br /><br />BAD GIRLS -- MISS<br />Here's another remix where the music is competing with the vocals, even though it starts off with a promising beat. I think the music sounds like a cross between the high energy/freeestyle 80s songs \\"Don Qichotte,\\" \\"Wake Up,\\" and \\"So Many Men, So Little Time,\\" all on [[ASIN:B000003Z9I TSR Retro Dance Classics]]. And the iconic \\"Toot, toot! Hey, beep, beep!\\" was left out until the very end, which is a let down.<br /><br />MACARTHUR PARK -- HIT<br />Updated musical embellishments, retains the original flavor, and has that big sound. The only thing missing would be the symphonic climax where the sound comes crashing down in the original; although this has the new improved build up, right at the moment of climax, it repeats rather than going over the top and cascading down. I don't even know how to put it in words, but fans of the original version will know what I'm talking about.<br /><br />I FEEL LOVE -- MISS<br />This remix is not as good as track 4. It loses its hypnotic power of the original and just sounds like some one-hit wonder from the 90s. This sounds like a few bars were taken from an 90s existing song (Robin S's \\"Show Me Love) and then overlaid over the original vocals and some instrumentation from \\"I Feel Love.\\" The only time it actually gets good is when you only hear the original.<br /><br />ON THE RADIO -- MISS<br />Although this track starts off with a promising drum beat (like the intro to Taana Gardner's \\"Heartbeat\\"), it starts to get murky and the different tempos make it less successful than it could be. If the remix used only the extremely slow ballad version of the vocals, like in the movie [[ASIN:B00009OWJT Foxes]], which used the ballad as interludes between scenes, this would have been a blistering remix: updated, fresh, and still hauntingly beautiful as the original used in the movie.<br /><br />LAST DANCE -- HIT<br />Ironically the musical track added to the Oscar winning song sounds more like an updated version of \\"Boogie Oogie Oogie\\" by  [[ASIN:B0040U8XR8 Taste of Honey]]. Despite this pairing, the song actually works. The vocals are intact as well as the violins and surprisingly, though the music and vocals are at different tempos at the beginning, the track still works. The only complaint I can find about this track is the repetition of the lines \\"Dance with me, dance with me, dance with me,\\" which is repeated as a segment five times. But I guess it was to build up anticipation for the payoff.<br /><br />LA DOLCE VITA -- HIT<br />I've never heard this song before, so I'm not sure if it was a previously unreleased track, but I like it. It does sound like there's a filter used on her voice, so it has a slight computer/artificial sound to it, but it's still pretty good. The filter makes her sound a little like Nina Simone with higher octave and range.<br /><br />The CD presentation is less than stellar. There's no booklet and the liner notes are sparse to say the least, which are behind the CD on the cardboard case. No pictures included either. Universal should take a cue from BIG BREAK RECORDS that does incredible reissues with booklets, pictures etc., but I guess it's this division of Universal -- the Verve imprint -- because Hip-O Select does a decent job with the presentation of reissues and I believe they're also part of the Universal Music Group.<br /><br />These are not better than the original releases of these songs, nor do I think that was the goal of this CD. They are different and vary from interpretation to interpretation from DJ/Producer to DJ/Producer, and I believe that it was done more as a homage not only to Donna Summer, but also to Giorgio Moroder and Pete Bellote. There's not a great DJ out there today who can say that they weren't inspiredor influenced by this team's contribution to dance music -- regardless of age or what genre they might be spinning now.<br /><br />Anyway, if you're looking for the originals might I suggest either [[ASIN:B0000DZP5X Ultimate Collection]], 3-CD set which includes all of Summer's hits including those from different record labels and has the 12\\" version of \\"Love To Love (Baby)\\" (that was only available on the album) or [[ASIN:B00009U5J5 Bad Girls]], the deluxe 2-CD set includes the multi-platinum album \\"BAD GIRLS,\\" and features the demo version of title song. The second CD has the 12\\" (extended play) of the monster hits (this CD is basically remastered version of [[ASIN:B000001FKP The Dance Collection]] minus the song \\"With Your Love,\\" but includes 12-inch \\"Bad Girls\\" and \\"On The Radio\\" long version.<br /><br />I'm sure fans will already know this, but Universal Japan has released all of Summer's Casablanca albums on Mini-Album CDs with 24-bit remastering. The one I would definitely recommend from that lot is [[ASIN:B008861TWQ Live & More]]. This reissue includes the original album fold and the inclusion of the nearly 18-minute opus, \\"MacArthur Park Suite.\\" I bought mine from Amazon Japan, which was much cheaper (at the time) than buying as an import from Amazon USA, even with shipping.: updated, fresh, and still hauntingly beautiful as the original used in the movie.<br /><br />LAST DANCE -- HIT<br />Ironically the musical track added to the Oscar winning song sounds more like an updated version of \\"Boogie Oogie Oogie\\" by  [[ASIN:B0040U8XR8 Taste of Honey]]. I do plan on seeing the movies, unlike a lot of other fans. B0017SWPXY|5.0|I found the characters and story of The Art of Racing in the Rain real, relateable, and poignant. As other reviews say, the book is told through the eyes and mind of a dog who has attained the mental facilities of a human. If you cannot get past that aspect of the story, then this book is simply not for you. If you have the imagination to suspend your disbelief, then read-on.<br /><br />Having no prior interest in this book other than it was recommended by a server while I was eating lunch at a CPK in Pleasanton, CA, I was completely surprised and pleased to depart from my normal diet of sci-fi and fantasy.<br /><br />Without spoilers, our narrator, Enzo the dog, observes his family interactions and relates different events to his love of racing. I couldn't care much less about racing (either human- or machine-powered) so I was skeptical about picking this book off the shelf. Once I did, I was so very pleased I set aside my prejudices.<br /><br />Some will call this book emotion porn, and I agree to some point. The story is certainly written to evoke intense emotions. I also feel that real life can be equally emotional and I think the milestones of the story are also very well-told. One of the most profound ideas in this book is \\"your car goes where your eyes go\\". It makes sense when you read the story. I don't think anyone should read too much into the ideas of The Art of Racing in the Rain, but consider them as reinforcement of good mental exercises.

B009VIZDLI|5.0|You will want to hold this one in your hands. Buy it and read it carefully and give it to someone you like for Xmas. Actually, you will want to keep it, so you have it on hand so you can give it to someone spontaneously. You will have to buy more copies for Xmas and birthday gifts.  This book is a joy to read, a joy to have and a joy to give.  It is lovely. Not a profound exploration of the life of someone with autism, and via this of the human condition. But an empathetic view of the world as seen through the eyes a someone at the high-functioning end of the spectrum - and a wonderful portrayal of him as seen by other people. The author has done his research, but treats his topic lightly and lovingly, through humour. The ending is possibly a bit schmaltzy, but I don't see how this story could have finished in any other way. This is a feel-good book, a clever, insightful, beautifully written and very funny feel-good book, and it would be a betrayal of the genre and more importantly of the reader to end it in any other way.
